airliner</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Variants"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Variants</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Overview"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Overview</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#A320"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">A320</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#A321"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">A321</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#A319"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">A319</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-30"><a href="#ACJ319"><span class="tocnumber">4.4.1</span> <span class="toctext">ACJ319</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#A318"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">A318</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Freighter_conversion"><span class="tocnumber">4.6</span> <span class="toctext">Freighter conversion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Airborne_Early_Warning_conversion"><span class="tocnumber">4.7</span> <span class="toctext">Airborne Early Warning conversion</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Operators"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Operators</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Orders_and_deliveries"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Orders and deliveries</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Accidents_and_incidents"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Accidents and incidents</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Specifications"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Specifications</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Engines_2"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Engines</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joint_European_Transport_JET2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/73/Joint_European_Transport_JET2.jpg/220px-Joint_European_Transport_JET2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="92" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/73/Joint_European_Transport_JET2.jpg/330px-Joint_European_Transport_JET2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/73/Joint_European_Transport_JET2.jpg/440px-Joint_European_Transport_JET2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="208" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joint_European_Transport_JET2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Joint European Transport JET2 concept</div></div></div>\n<p>When <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a> designed the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A300" title="Airbus A300">A300</a> during the late 1960s and early 1970s, it envisaged a broad family of airliners with which to compete against <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Aircraft_Company" title="Douglas Aircraft Company">Douglas</a>, two established US aerospace manufacturers. From the moment of formation, Airbus had begun studies into derivatives of the Airbus A300B in support of this long-term goal.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the service introduction of the first Airbus airliners, engineers within Airbus had identified nine possible variations of the A300 known as A300B1 to B9.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> A 10th variation, conceived in 1973, later the first to be constructed, was designated the A300B10.<sup id="cite_ref-N&amp;W_p.18_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-N&amp;W_p.18-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It was a smaller aircraft which would be developed into the long-range <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A310" title="Airbus A310">Airbus A310</a>. Airbus then focused its efforts on the single-aisle market, which was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">737</a> and <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-9" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-9">McDonnell Douglas DC-9</a>.\n</p><p>Plans from a number of European aircraft manufacturers called for a successor to the relatively successful <a href="/wiki/BAC_One-Eleven" title="BAC One-Eleven">BAC One-Eleven</a>, and to replace the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737-200" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 737-200">737-200</a> and DC-9.<sup id="cite_ref-FG.com_A320_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG.com_A320-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Germany\'s MBB (<i><a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt-B%C3%B6lkow-Blohm" title="Messerschmitt-B\xc3\xb6lkow-Blohm">Messerschmitt-B\xc3\xb6lkow-Blohm</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/British_Aircraft_Corporation" title="British Aircraft Corporation">British Aircraft Corporation</a>, Sweden\'s <a href="/wiki/Saab_AB" title="Saab AB">Saab</a> and Spain\'s <a href="/wiki/EADS_CASA" class="mw-redirect" title="EADS CASA">CASA</a> worked on the <i>EUROPLANE</i>, a 180- to 200-seat aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-FG.com_A320_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG.com_A320-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It was abandoned after intruding on A310 specifications.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/VFW-Fokker" title="VFW-Fokker">VFW-Fokker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dornier_Flugzeugwerke" title="Dornier Flugzeugwerke">Dornier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley" title="Hawker Siddeley">Hawker Siddeley</a> worked on a number of 150-seat designs.<sup id="cite_ref-FG.com_A320_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG.com_A320-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The design within the JET study that was carried forward was the JET2 (163 passengers), which then became the Airbus S.A1/2/3 series (Single Aisle), before settling on the A320 name for its launch in 1984. Previously, Hawker Siddeley had produced a design called the HS.134 "Airbus" in 1965, an evolution of the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley_Trident" title="Hawker Siddeley Trident">HS.121</a> (formerly DH.121) <i>Trident</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> which shared much of the general arrangement of the later JET3 study design. The name "Airbus" at the time referred to a <a href="/wiki/British_European_Airways" title="British European Airways">BEA</a> requirement, rather than to the later international programme.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_effort">Design effort</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Design effort">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:VietJet_Air_Interior_in_A320-family_airframes_-_50899336336.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/VietJet_Air_Interior_in_A320-family_airframes_-_50899336336.jpg/220px-VietJet_Air_Interior_in_A320-family_airframes_-_50899336336.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/VietJet_Air_Interior_in_A320-family_airframes_-_50899336336.jpg/330px-VietJet_Air_Interior_in_A320-family_airframes_-_50899336336.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/VietJet_Air_Interior_in_A320-family_airframes_-_50899336336.jpg/440px-VietJet_Air_Interior_in_A320-family_airframes_-_50899336336.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VietJet_Air_Interior_in_A320-family_airframes_-_50899336336.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The six-abreast cabin cross section</div></div></div>\n<p>In June 1977 a new <i>Joint European Transport</i> (JET) programme was set up, established by <a href="/wiki/British_Aerospace" title="British Aerospace">British Aerospace</a> (BAe), <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9rospatiale" title="A\xc3\xa9rospatiale">Aerospatiale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dornier_Flugzeugwerke" title="Dornier Flugzeugwerke">Dornier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fokker" title="Fokker">Fokker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It was based at the then BAe (formerly <a href="/wiki/Vickers" title="Vickers">Vickers</a>) site in <a href="/wiki/Weybridge" title="Weybridge">Weybridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>, UK. Although the members were all of Airbus\' partners, they regarded the project as a separate collaboration from Airbus.<sup id="cite_ref-Aris_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aris-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> This project was considered the forerunner of Airbus A320, encompassing the 130- to 188-seat market, powered by two <a href="/wiki/CFM56" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM56">CFM56s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FG.com_A320_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG.com_A320-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It would have a cruise speed of Mach 0.84 (faster than the Boeing 737).<sup id="cite_ref-FG.com_A320_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG.com_A320-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The programme was later transferred to Airbus, leading up to the creation of the <i>Single-Aisle</i> (SA) studies in 1980, led by former leader of JET programme, Derek Brown.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The group looked at three different variants, covering the 125- to 180-seat market, called <i>SA1</i>, <i>SA2</i> and <i>SA3</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FG.com_A320_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG.com_A320-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Although unaware at the time, the consortium was producing the blueprints for the A319, A320 and A321, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The single-aisle programme created divisions within Airbus about whether to design a shorter-range twinjet rather than a longer-range quadjet wanted by the West Germans, particularly <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FG.com_A320_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG.com_A320-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aris_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aris-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, works proceeded, and the German carrier would eventually order the twinjet.\n</p><p>In February 1981 the project was re-designated A320,<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> with efforts focused on the former SA2. During the year, Airbus worked with <a href="/wiki/Delta_Air_Lines" title="Delta Air Lines">Delta Air Lines</a> on a 150-seat aircraft envisioned and required by the airline. The A320 would carry 150 passengers over 5,280 or 3,440&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a> (2,850 or 1,860&#160;nmi) using fuel from wing fuel tanks only.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The -200 had the centre tank activated, increasing fuel capacity from 15,590 to 23,430&#160;L (3,429 to 5,154&#160;imp&#160;gal).<sup id="cite_ref-Eden_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eden-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> They would measure 36.04 and 39.24&#160;m (118&#160;ft 3&#160;in and 128&#160;ft 9&#160;in), respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Airbus considered a fuselage diameter of "the Boeing 707 and 727, or do something better" and settled on a wider cross-section with a 3.7&#160;m (12&#160;ft 2&#160;in) internal width, compared to Boeing\'s 3.45&#160;m (11&#160;ft 4&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-FG.com_A320_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG.com_A320-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Although heavier, this allowed to compete more effectively with the 737. The A320 wing went through several stages of design, finally settling on 33.91&#160;m (111&#160;ft 3&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Eden_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eden-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_shares">National shares</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: National shares">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The UK, France and <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> wanted the responsibility of final assembly and the associated duties, known as "work-share arguments". The Germans requested an increased work-share of 40%, while the British wanted the major responsibilities to be swapped around to give partners production and <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> experience. In the end, British work-share was increased from that of the two previous Airbuses.<sup id="cite_ref-Aris_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aris-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>France was willing to commit to a launch aid, or subsidies, while the Germans were more cautious.<sup id="cite_ref-Aris_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aris-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The UK government was unwilling to provide funding for the tooling requested by BAe and estimated at <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">\xc2\xa3</a>250&#160;million, it was postponed for three years.<sup id="cite_ref-Eden_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eden-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> On 1 March 1984 the government and the manufacturer agreed that \xc2\xa350&#160;million would be paid whether the A320 would fly or not, while the rest would be paid as a levy on each aircraft sold.<sup id="cite_ref-Aris_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aris-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\nIn 1984, the program cost was then estimated at \xc2\xa32 billion ($2.8 billion) by Flight International.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> ot \xc2\xa36 billion today.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:F-WWAI_A320-131_Airbus_Industrie(prototype)_FAB_SEP88_(13753510323).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/F-WWAI_A320-131_Airbus_Industrie%28prototype%29_FAB_SEP88_%2813753510323%29.jpg/220px-F-WWAI_A320-131_Airbus_Industrie%28prototype%29_FAB_SEP88_%2813753510323%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/F-WWAI_A320-131_Airbus_Industrie%28prototype%29_FAB_SEP88_%2813753510323%29.jpg/330px-F-WWAI_A320-131_Airbus_Industrie%28prototype%29_FAB_SEP88_%2813753510323%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/F-WWAI_A320-131_Airbus_Industrie%28prototype%29_FAB_SEP88_%2813753510323%29.jpg/440px-F-WWAI_A320-131_Airbus_Industrie%28prototype%29_FAB_SEP88_%2813753510323%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2197" data-file-height="1465" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:F-WWAI_A320-131_Airbus_Industrie(prototype)_FAB_SEP88_(13753510323).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The A320 first prototype at the 1988 Farnborough Airshow</div></div></div>\n<p>The programme was launched on 2 March 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> At this time, Airbus had 96 orders.<sup id="cite_ref-NW1999_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NW1999-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>48</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Air_France" title="Air France">Air France</a> was its first customer with a "letter of intent" for 25 A320s and an option for 25 more at the 1981 <a href="/wiki/Paris_air_show" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris air show">Paris air show</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In October 1983, <a href="/wiki/British_Caledonian" title="British Caledonian">British Caledonian</a> placed seven firm orders, bringing total orders to more than 80.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_Airways" title="Cyprus Airways">Cyprus Airways</a> became the first to place order for V2500-powered A320s in November 1984, followed by <a href="/wiki/Pan_American_World_Airways" class="mw-redirect" title="Pan American World Airways">Pan Am</a> with 16 firm orders and 34 options in January 1985, then <a href="/wiki/Inex_Adria" class="mw-redirect" title="Inex Adria">Inex Adria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NW1999_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NW1999-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>49</span></sup> One of the most significant orders was when <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Airlines" title="Northwest Airlines">Northwest Airlines</a> placed an order for 100 A320s in October 1986, later confirmed at the 1990 Farnborough Airshow, powered by CFM56 engines.<sup id="cite_ref-NW1999_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NW1999-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>49\xe2\x80\x9350</span></sup>\n</p><p>During the A320 development programme, Airbus considered <a href="/wiki/Propfan" title="Propfan">propfan</a> technology, backed by <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Aris_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aris-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> At the time unproven, it was essentially a fan placed outside the engine <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelle</a>, offering speed of a turbofan at <a href="/wiki/Turboprop" title="Turboprop">turboprops</a> economics; eventually, Airbus stuck with turbofans.\n</p><p>Power on the A320 would be supplied by two CFM56-5-A1s rated at 25,000&#160;lbf (111.2&#160;kN).<sup id="cite_ref-Eden_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eden-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It was the only available engine at launch until the <a href="/wiki/IAE_V2500" title="IAE V2500">IAE V2500</a>, offered by <a href="/wiki/International_Aero_Engines" title="International Aero Engines">International Aero Engines</a>, a group composed of <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_plc" class="mw-redirect" title="Rolls-Royce plc">Rolls-Royce plc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney">Pratt &amp; Whitney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Aero_Engine_Corporation" title="Japanese Aero Engine Corporation">Japanese Aero Engine Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiat" title="Fiat">Fiat</a> and <a href="/wiki/MTU_Aero_Engines" title="MTU Aero Engines">MTU</a>. The first V2500 variant, the V2500-A1, has a thrust output of 25,000 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">pounds-force</a> (110&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> hence the name. It is 4% more efficient than the CFM56, with cruise <a href="/wiki/Thrust_specific_fuel_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="Thrust specific fuel consumption">thrust specific fuel consumption</a> for the -A5 at 0.574 and 0.596&#160;lb/lbf/h (16.3 and 16.9&#160;g/kN/s) for the CFM56-5A1.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A320-100_Air_France_(AFR)_F-GFKQ_-_MSN_002_(10655931213).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Airbus_A320-100_Air_France_%28AFR%29_F-GFKQ_-_MSN_002_%2810655931213%29.jpg/220px-Airbus_A320-100_Air_France_%28AFR%29_F-GFKQ_-_MSN_002_%2810655931213%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Airbus_A320-100_Air_France_%28AFR%29_F-GFKQ_-_MSN_002_%2810655931213%29.jpg/330px-Airbus_A320-100_Air_France_%28AFR%29_F-GFKQ_-_MSN_002_%2810655931213%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Airbus_A320-100_Air_France_%28AFR%29_F-GFKQ_-_MSN_002_%2810655931213%29.jpg/440px-Airbus_A320-100_Air_France_%28AFR%29_F-GFKQ_-_MSN_002_%2810655931213%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2989" data-file-height="1992" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A320-100_Air_France_(AFR)_F-GFKQ_-_MSN_002_(10655931213).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first A320 was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Air_France" title="Air France">Air France</a> on 28 March 1988, the early A320-100 has no <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device#Wingtip_fence" title="Wingtip device">wingtip fences</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the presence of then French Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">Jacques Chirac</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Prince</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Diana, Princess of Wales">Princess of Wales</a>, the first A320 was rolled out of the final assembly line on 14 February 1987 and made its <a href="/wiki/Maiden_flight" title="Maiden flight">maiden flight</a> on 22 February in 3 hours and 23 minutes from Toulouse.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The flight test programme took 1,200 hours on 530 flights. European <a href="/wiki/Joint_Aviation_Authorities" title="Joint Aviation Authorities">Joint Aviation Authorities</a> certification was delivered on 26 February 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-NW1999_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NW1999-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>50</span></sup> The first A320 was delivered to Air France on 28 March 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\nIn 1988, the program cost was 5.486 Bn <a href="/wiki/French_franc" title="French franc">FRF</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stretching_the_A320:_A321">Stretching the A320: A321</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Stretching the A320: A321">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:D-AIRN_(7065101303).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/D-AIRN_%287065101303%29.jpg/220px-D-AIRN_%287065101303%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/D-AIRN_%287065101303%29.jpg/330px-D-AIRN_%287065101303%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/D-AIRN_%287065101303%29.jpg/440px-D-AIRN_%287065101303%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:D-AIRN_(7065101303).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a> was the first to receive the stretched A321 on 27 January 1994<sup id="cite_ref-Eden_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eden-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A321#Development" title="Airbus A321">Airbus A321, Development</a></div>\n<p>The first derivative of the A320 was the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A321" title="Airbus A321">Airbus A321</a>, also known as the <i>Stretched A320</i>, <i>A320-500</i> and <i>A325</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Its launch came on 24 November 1988 after commitments for 183 aircraft from 10 customers were secured.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reed_p.84_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reed_p.84-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The aircraft would be a minimum-changed derivative, apart from a number of minor modifications to the wing, and the fuselage stretch itself. The wing would incorporate <a href="/wiki/Flap_(aircraft)" class="mw-redirect" title="Flap (aircraft)">double-slotted flaps</a> and minor <a href="/wiki/Trailing_edge" title="Trailing edge">trailing edge</a> modifications,<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> increasing the wing area from 124&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (1,330&#160;sq&#160;ft) to 128&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (1,380&#160;sq&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-A321_described_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A321_described-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The fuselage was lengthened by four plugs (two ahead and two behind the wings), giving the A321 an overall length of 6.94 metres (22&#160;ft 9&#160;in) longer than the A320.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A320_specifications_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A320_specifications-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A321_specifications_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A321_specifications-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The length increase required the overwing exits of the A320 to be enlarged and repositioned in front of and behind the wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Eden_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eden-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The centre fuselage and <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">undercarriage</a> were reinforced to accommodate the increase in <a href="/wiki/Maximum_takeoff_weight" title="Maximum takeoff weight">maximum takeoff weight</a> of 9,600&#160;kg (21,200&#160;lb), taking it to 83,000&#160;kg (183,000&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Final assembly for the A321 would be, as a first for any Airbus, carried out in Germany (then West Germany).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> This came after a dispute between the French, who claimed the move would incur $150&#160;million (\xe2\x82\xac135 million) in unnecessary expenditure associated with the new plant,<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and the Germans, arguing it would be more productive for Airbus in the long run. The second production line was located at <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, which would also subsequently produce the smaller Airbus A319 and A318. For the first time, Airbus entered the <a href="/wiki/Bond_market" title="Bond market">bond market</a>, through which it raised $480&#160;million (\xe2\x82\xac475 million) to finance development costs.<sup id="cite_ref-Reed_p.84_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reed_p.84-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> An additional $180&#160;million (\xe2\x82\xac175 million) was borrowed from <a href="/wiki/European_Investment_Bank" title="European Investment Bank">European Investment Bank</a> and private investors.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The maiden flight of the Airbus A321 came on 11 March 1993, when the <a href="/wiki/Prototype" title="Prototype">prototype</a>, registration F-WWIA, flew with IAE V2500 engines; the second prototype, equipped with CFM56-5B turbofans, flew in May. Lufthansa and <a href="/wiki/Alitalia" title="Alitalia">Alitalia</a> were the first to order the stretched Airbuses, with 20 and 40 aircraft requested, respectively. The first of Lufthansa\'s V2500-A5-powered A321s arrived on 27 January 1994, while Alitalia received its first CFM56-5B-powered aircraft on 22 March.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shrinking_the_A320:_A319">Shrinking the A320: A319</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Shrinking the A320: A319">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1ak_-_Swissair_Airbus_A319-112;_HB-IPX@ZRH;06.12.1997_(4704674814).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/1ak_-_Swissair_Airbus_A319-112%3B_HB-IPX%40ZRH%3B06.12.1997_%284704674814%29.jpg/220px-1ak_-_Swissair_Airbus_A319-112%3B_HB-IPX%40ZRH%3B06.12.1997_%284704674814%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/1ak_-_Swissair_Airbus_A319-112%3B_HB-IPX%40ZRH%3B06.12.1997_%284704674814%29.jpg/330px-1ak_-_Swissair_Airbus_A319-112%3B_HB-IPX%40ZRH%3B06.12.1997_%284704674814%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/1ak_-_Swissair_Airbus_A319-112%3B_HB-IPX%40ZRH%3B06.12.1997_%284704674814%29.jpg/440px-1ak_-_Swissair_Airbus_A319-112%3B_HB-IPX%40ZRH%3B06.12.1997_%284704674814%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4107" data-file-height="2813" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1ak_-_Swissair_Airbus_A319-112;_HB-IPX@ZRH;06.12.1997_(4704674814).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first A319 was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Swissair" title="Swissair">Swissair</a> on 25 April 1996</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A319#Development" title="Airbus A319">Airbus A319, Development</a></div>\n<p>The A319 is the next derivative of the baseline A320. The design is a "shrink" with its origins in the 130- to 140-seat SA1, part of the Single-Aisle studies.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The SA1 was shelved as the consortium concentrated on its bigger siblings. After healthy sales of the A320/A321, Airbus re-focused on what was then known as the <i>A320M-7</i>, meaning A320 <i>minus seven fuselage frames</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Eden_15-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eden-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It would provide direct competition for the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737-300" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 737-300">737-300</a>/<a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">-700</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The shrink was achieved through the removal of four fuselage frames fore and three aft of the wing, cutting the overall length by 3.73 metres (12&#160;ft 3&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-A320_specifications_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A320_specifications-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meeting_demand_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meeting_demand-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A319_specifications_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A319_specifications-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, the number of overwing exits was reduced from four to two. The bulk-cargo door was replaced by an aft container door, which can take in reduced height <a href="/wiki/Unit_Load_Device" class="mw-redirect" title="Unit Load Device">LD3-45 containers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Meeting_demand_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meeting_demand-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Minor <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">software</a> changes were made to accommodate the different handling characteristics; otherwise the aircraft is largely unchanged. Power is provided by the CFM56-5A or V2500-A5, derated to 98&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a> (22,000&#160;lbf), with option for 105&#160;kN (24,000&#160;lbf) thrust.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Airbus began offering the new model from 22 May 1992, with the actual launch of the $275&#160;million (\xe2\x82\xac250 million) programme occurring on 10 June 1993;<sup id="cite_ref-Meeting_demand_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meeting_demand-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> the A319\'s first customer was <a href="/wiki/International_Lease_Finance_Corporation" title="International Lease Finance Corporation">ILFC</a>, who signed for six aircraft. On 23 March 1995, the first A319 underwent final assembly at Airbus\' German plant in Hamburg, where the A321s are also assembled. It was rolled out on 24 August 1995, with the maiden flight the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-Eden_15-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eden-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The certification programme would take 350 airborne hours involving two aircraft; certification for the CFM56-5B6/2-equipped variant was granted in April 1996, after which qualification for the V2524-A5 started the following month.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Delivery of the first A319, to <a href="/wiki/Swissair" title="Swissair">Swissair</a>, took place on 25 April 1996, entering service by month\'s end.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In January 1997, an A319 broke a record during a delivery flight by flying 3,588 nautical miles (6,645&#160;km) the <a href="/wiki/Great_circle" title="Great circle">great circle</a> route to <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg" title="Winnipeg">Winnipeg</a>, Manitoba from Hamburg, in 9 hours 5 minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The A319 has proved popular with low-cost airlines such as <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">EasyJet</a>, which purchased 172 of them.<sup id="cite_ref-Airbus_Orders_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Airbus_Orders-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_shrink:_A318">Second shrink: A318</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Second shrink: A318">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A318#Development" title="Airbus A318">Airbus A318, Development</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:N806FR_at_LAX.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/N806FR_at_LAX.jpg/220px-N806FR_at_LAX.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/N806FR_at_LAX.jpg/330px-N806FR_at_LAX.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/N806FR_at_LAX.jpg/440px-N806FR_at_LAX.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="844" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:N806FR_at_LAX.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Frontier_Airlines" title="Frontier Airlines">Frontier Airlines</a> received the first A318 on 22 July 2003</div></div></div>\n<p>The A318 was born out of mid-1990 studies between <a href="/wiki/Aviation_Industry_Corporation_of_China" title="Aviation Industry Corporation of China">Aviation Industry Corporation of China</a> (AVIC), <a href="/wiki/ST_Aerospace" title="ST Aerospace">Singapore Technologies Aerospace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alenia_Aeronautica" title="Alenia Aeronautica">Alenia</a> and Airbus on a 95- to 125-seat aircraft project. The programme was called the <i>AE31X</i>, and covered the 95-seat AE316 and 115- to 125-seat AE317.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The former would have had an overall length of 31.3&#160;m (102&#160;ft 8&#160;in), while the AE317 was longer by 3.2&#160;m (10&#160;ft 6&#160;in), at 34.5&#160;m (113&#160;ft 2&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-AE31X_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AE31X-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The engines were to be supplied from two Rolls-Royce BR715s, CFM56-9s, or the <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_PW6000" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney PW6000">Pratt &amp; Whitney PW6000</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AE31X_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AE31X-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> with the MTOW of 53.3&#160;t (118,000&#160;lb) for the smaller version and 58&#160;t (128,000&#160;lb) for the AE317, the thrust requirement were 77.9\xe2\x80\x9384.6&#160;kN (17,500\xe2\x80\x9319,000&#160;lbf) and 84.6\xe2\x80\x9391.2&#160;kN (19,000\xe2\x80\x9320,500&#160;lbf), respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-AE31X_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AE31X-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Range was settled at 5,200&#160;km (2,800&#160;nmi) and 5,800&#160;km (3,100&#160;nmi) for the high gross weights of both variants.<sup id="cite_ref-AE31X_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AE31X-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Both share a wingspan of 31.0&#160;m (101&#160;ft 8&#160;in)<sup id="cite_ref-AE31X_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AE31X-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Cockpit" title="Cockpit">flight deck</a> similar to that of the A320 family. Costing $2&#160;billion (\xe2\x82\xac1.85 billion) to develop, aircraft production was to take place in China.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Simultaneously, Airbus was developing Airbus A318. In early 1998, Airbus revealed its considerations of designing a 100-seat aircraft based on the A320. The AE31X project was terminated by September 1998, after which Airbus officially announced an aircraft of its own, the <i>A318</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> at that year\'s Farnborough Airshow.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The aircraft is the smallest product of Airbus\'s product range, and was developed coincidentally at the same time as the largest commercial aircraft in history, the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380</a>. First called <i>A319M5</i> in as early as March 1995, it was shorter by 0.79-metre (2&#160;ft 7&#160;in) ahead of the wing and 1.6 metres (5&#160;ft 3&#160;in) behind.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> These cuts reduced passenger capacity from 124 on the A319 to 107 passengers in a two-class layout.<sup id="cite_ref-A318_specifications_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A318_specifications-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Range was 5,700 kilometres (3,100&#160;nmi), or 5,950 kilometres (3,210&#160;nmi) with upcoming <a href="/wiki/Sharklet_(wingtip_device)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharklet (wingtip device)">Sharklets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-A318_specifications_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A318_specifications-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 107-seater was launched on 26 April 1999 with the options and orders count at 109 aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> After three years of design, the maiden flight took place at Hamburg on 15 January 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Tests on the lead engine, the PW6000, revealed worse-than-expected fuel consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Minibus_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minibus-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, Pratt &amp; Whitney abandoned the five-stage high-pressure compressor (HPC) for the MTU-designed six-stage HPC. The 129 order book for the A318 shrunk to 80 largely because of switches to other A320 family members.<sup id="cite_ref-Minibus_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minibus-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> After 17 months of flight certification, during which 850 hours and 350 flights were accumulated, JAA certification was obtained for the CFM56-powered variant on 23 May 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-Minibus_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minibus-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> On 22 July 2003, first delivery for launch customer <a href="/wiki/Frontier_Airlines" title="Frontier Airlines">Frontier Airlines</a> occurred,<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> entering service before the end of the month.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A321_final_assembly_(9351765668).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/A321_final_assembly_%289351765668%29.jpg/220px-A321_final_assembly_%289351765668%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/A321_final_assembly_%289351765668%29.jpg/330px-A321_final_assembly_%289351765668%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/A321_final_assembly_%289351765668%29.jpg/440px-A321_final_assembly_%289351765668%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A321_final_assembly_(9351765668).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Airbus A321 on final <a href="/wiki/Assembly_line" title="Assembly line">assembly line</a> 3 in the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Hamburg-Finkenwerder" title="Airbus Hamburg-Finkenwerder">Airbus Hamburg-Finkenwerder</a> plant</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Toulouse_Blagnac_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Toulouse Blagnac International Airport">Toulouse Blagnac</a> final assembly line builds A320s, whereas the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Hamburg-Finkenwerder" title="Airbus Hamburg-Finkenwerder">Hamburg Finkenwerder</a> final assembly line builds A318s, A319s, and A321s. The Airbus factory in <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a>, China assembles A319s, A320s, and A321s; A320s and A321s are also assembled at the Airbus Americas factory in <a href="/wiki/Mobile,_Alabama" title="Mobile, Alabama">Mobile, Alabama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Airbus produced a total of 42 A320s per month in 2015, and expects to increase to 50 per month in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-osb1_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osb1-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As Airbus targets a 60 monthly global production rate by mid-2019, the Tianjin line delivered 51 in 2016 and it could assemble six per month from four as it starts producing A320neos in 2017; 147 Airbus were delivered in 2016 in China, 20% of its production, mostly A320-family, a 47% market share as the country should become the world\'s largest market ahead of the US before 2027.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 2018, along a larger and modernised delivery centre, Airbus inaugurated its fourth Hamburg production line, with two seven-axis <a href="/wiki/Industrial_robot" title="Industrial robot">robots</a> to drill 80% of fuselage upper side holes, <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous vehicle">autonomous</a> mobile tooling platforms and following <a href="/wiki/Design_Thinking" class="mw-redirect" title="Design Thinking">Design Thinking</a> principles.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> By January 2019, Mobile was outputting 4.5 A320s per month, raising to five by the end of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-avweek-20190117_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-avweek-20190117-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2019, Airbus reached a milestone with the delivery of the 9000th A320-family aircraft to Easyjet. In October 2019, Airbus inaugurated a highly automated fuselage structure assembly line for A320 Family aircraft in Hamburg, showcasing an evolution in Airbus\' industrial production system.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Production rates continue to rise, and Airbus aims to reach a production rate of 63 aircraft per month by 2021, which would result in the 10,000th delivery occurring early that year.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to the <a href="/wiki/Impact_of_the_COVID-19_pandemic_on_aviation" title="Impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on aviation">impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on aviation</a>, demand for new jets was reduced in 2020 and Airbus cut its monthly production from 60 to 40 A320s.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight27oct2020_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight27oct2020-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\nIn October 2020, the 500th A320 built in Tianjin, an A320neo, was delivered to <a href="/wiki/China_Southern" class="mw-redirect" title="China Southern">China Southern</a>, twelve years after the final assembly line start in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A320_Enhanced">A320 Enhanced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: A320 Enhanced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Improvements">Improvements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Improvements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 2006, Airbus started the A320 Enhanced (A320E) programme as a series of improvements targeting a 4\xe2\x80\x935% efficiency gain with large <a href="/wiki/Winglet" class="mw-redirect" title="Winglet">winglets</a> (2%), aerodynamic refinements (1%), weight savings and a new <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_cabin" title="Aircraft cabin">aircraft cabin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight20jun2006_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight20jun2006-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\nEngine improvements reducing fuel consumption by 1% were fitted into the A320 in 2007 with the <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56#Tech56_and_Tech_Insertion" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM56 Tech Insertion</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and in 2008 with the <a href="/wiki/V2500#V2500SelectOne" class="mw-redirect" title="V2500">V2500Select (One)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sharklets">Sharklets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sharklets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sharklet_of_F-WWIQ_Airbus_A320_ILA_2012_05.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sharklet_of_F-WWIQ_Airbus_A320_ILA_2012_05.jpg/170px-Sharklet_of_F-WWIQ_Airbus_A320_ILA_2012_05.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sharklet_of_F-WWIQ_Airbus_A320_ILA_2012_05.jpg/255px-Sharklet_of_F-WWIQ_Airbus_A320_ILA_2012_05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sharklet_of_F-WWIQ_Airbus_A320_ILA_2012_05.jpg/340px-Sharklet_of_F-WWIQ_Airbus_A320_ILA_2012_05.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1860" data-file-height="2800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sharklet_of_F-WWIQ_Airbus_A320_ILA_2012_05.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wingtip sharklet</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2006, Airbus tested three styles of winglet intended to counteract the wing\'s <a href="/wiki/Induced_drag" class="mw-redirect" title="Induced drag">induced drag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_vortices" title="Wingtip vortices">wingtip vortices</a> more effectively than the previous wingtip fence. The first design type to be tested was developed by Airbus and was based on work done by the <abbr title="Aircraft wing advanced technology operations">AWIATOR</abbr> programme.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The second type of winglet incorporated a more blended design and was designed by Winglet Technology, a company based in <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">Wichita</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, USA. Two aircraft were used in the flight test evaluation campaign&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 the prototype A320, which had been retained by Airbus for testing, and a new build aircraft which was fitted with both types of winglets before it was delivered to <a href="/wiki/JetBlue" title="JetBlue">JetBlue</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Despite the anticipated efficiency gains and development work, Airbus announced that the new winglets will not be offered to customers, claiming that the weight of the modifications required would negate any aerodynamic benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-fi_20061010_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fi_20061010-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> On 17 December 2008, Airbus announced it was to begin flight testing an existing blended winglet design developed by <a href="/wiki/Aviation_Partners_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Aviation Partners Inc.">Aviation Partners Inc.</a> as part of an A320 modernisation programme using the A320 prototype.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Airbus launched the sharklet blended <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">wingtip device</a> during the November 2009 <a href="/wiki/Dubai_Airshow" title="Dubai Airshow">Dubai Airshow</a>: installation adds 200&#160;kg (440&#160;lb) but offers a 3.5% <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_aircraft" title="Fuel economy in aircraft">fuel burn</a> reduction on flights over 2,800&#160;km (1,500&#160;nmi).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> They save US$220,000 and 700 t of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> per aircraft per year.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The 2.5 metres (8&#160;ft 2&#160;in) tall wingtip devices are manufactured by <a href="/wiki/Korean_Air" title="Korean Air">Korean Air</a> Aerospace Division.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The winglets increase efficiency by decreasing <a href="/wiki/Lift-induced_drag" title="Lift-induced drag">lift-induced drag</a>, thereby offering more range.\n</p><p>In December 2011, Airbus filed suit in the western district of Texas over <a href="/wiki/Aviation_Partners" title="Aviation Partners">Aviation Partners</a>\' claims of infringement of its <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patents</a> on winglet design and construction which were granted in 1993. Airbus\' lawsuit seeks to reject responsibility to pay <a href="/wiki/Royalties" class="mw-redirect" title="Royalties">royalties</a> to Aviation Partners for using its designs, despite work performed together with both parties to develop advanced winglets for the Airbus A320neo.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first sharklet-equipped Airbus A320 was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Indonesia_AirAsia" title="Indonesia AirAsia">Indonesia AirAsia</a> on 21 December 2012, offering a 450&#160;kg (990&#160;lb) payload increase and 190&#160;km (100&#160;nmi) longer range at the original payload.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabin">Cabin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cabin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 2007, Airbus introduced a new enhanced, quieter <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_cabin" title="Aircraft cabin">cabin</a> with better luggage storage and a more modern look and feel, and a new galley reducing weight, increasing revenue space and improving ergonomics and design for food hygiene and recycling.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> It offers a new <a href="/wiki/Air_purifier" title="Air purifier">air purifier</a> with filters and a <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_converter" title="Catalytic converter">catalytic converter</a> removing unpleasant smells from the air before it is pumped into the cabin and <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">LEDs</a> for ambience lighting and <a href="/wiki/Passenger_service_unit" title="Passenger service unit">PSU</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Offering 10% more <a href="/wiki/Overhead_bin" class="mw-redirect" title="Overhead bin">overhead bin</a> volume, more shoulder room, a weight reduction, a new <a href="/wiki/Intercom" title="Intercom">intercom</a> and <a href="/wiki/In-flight_entertainment" title="In-flight entertainment">in-flight entertainment</a> system, noise reduction and slimmer PSU, the enhanced Cabin can be retrofitted.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The flight crew controls the cabin through touchscreen displays.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Engine_Option">New Engine Option<span class="anchor" id="New_Engine_Option"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: New Engine Option">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AIB_A320neo_F-WNEW_27may15_LFBO-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/AIB_A320neo_F-WNEW_27may15_LFBO-1.jpg/220px-AIB_A320neo_F-WNEW_27may15_LFBO-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/AIB_A320neo_F-WNEW_27may15_LFBO-1.jpg/330px-AIB_A320neo_F-WNEW_27may15_LFBO-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/AIB_A320neo_F-WNEW_27may15_LFBO-1.jpg/440px-AIB_A320neo_F-WNEW_27may15_LFBO-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5129" data-file-height="3323" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AIB_A320neo_F-WNEW_27may15_LFBO-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Airbus A320neo with larger engines and sharklets.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320neo_family" title="Airbus A320neo family">Airbus A320neo family</a></div>\n<p>The A320neo (<i>neo</i> for <i>new engine option</i>) is a development launched on 1 December 2010, it made its <a href="/wiki/First_flight" class="mw-redirect" title="First flight">first flight</a> on 25 September 2014 and it was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a> on 25 January 2016. <a href="/wiki/Re-engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Re-engine">Re-engined</a> with <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_LEAP" title="CFM International LEAP">CFM International LEAP</a>-1A or <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_PW1000G" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney PW1000G">Pratt &amp; Whitney PW1000G</a> engines and with large sharklets, it should be 15% more fuel efficient. Three variants are based on the previous A319, A320 and A321. Airbus received 6,031 orders by March 2018 and delivered 318 by May 2018. The original family is renamed A320ceo, for <i>current engine option</i>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iberia_Airbus_A320_EC-HAD.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Iberia_Airbus_A320_EC-HAD.jpg/220px-Iberia_Airbus_A320_EC-HAD.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Iberia_Airbus_A320_EC-HAD.jpg/330px-Iberia_Airbus_A320_EC-HAD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Iberia_Airbus_A320_EC-HAD.jpg/440px-Iberia_Airbus_A320_EC-HAD.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2678" data-file-height="1788" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iberia_Airbus_A320_EC-HAD.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Airbus A320 is a <a href="/wiki/Low_wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Low wing">low wing</a> airliner with <a href="/wiki/Twinjet" title="Twinjet">twin turbofans</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Conventional_tail" class="mw-redirect" title="Conventional tail">conventional tail</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Airbus A320 family are <a href="/wiki/Narrow-body_aircraft" title="Narrow-body aircraft">narrow-body (single-aisle) aircraft</a> with a retractable tricycle <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">landing gear</a> and are powered by two wing pylon-mounted <a href="/wiki/Turbofan_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Turbofan engine">turbofan engines</a>.\nAfter the <a href="/wiki/1970s_energy_crisis" title="1970s energy crisis">oil price rises of the 1970s</a>, Airbus needed to minimise the trip fuel costs of the A320. To that end, it adopted <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite</a> primary structures, <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">centre-of-gravity</a> control using fuel, <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a> (<a href="/wiki/Electronic_Flight_Information_Systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic Flight Information Systems">EFIS</a>) and a two-crew flight deck.\n</p><p>Airbus claimed the 737-300 burns 35% more fuel and has a 16% higher operating cost per seat than the <a href="/wiki/V2500" class="mw-redirect" title="V2500">V2500</a>-powered A320.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\nA 150-seat A320 burns 11,608&#160;kg (25,591&#160;lb) of <a href="/wiki/Aviation_fuel" title="Aviation fuel">jet fuel</a> over 3,984&#160;km (2,151&#160;nmi) (between Los Angeles and New York City), or 2.43&#160;L/100&#160;km (97&#160;mpg<sub>&#8209;US</sub>) per seat with a 0.8&#160;kg/L fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-SEI200805_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEI200805-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\nIts wing is long and thin, offering better aerodynamic efficiency because of the higher <a href="/wiki/Aspect_ratio_(wing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspect ratio (wing)">aspect ratio</a> than the competing 737 and MD-80.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airframe">Airframe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Airframe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iberia_a320-200_planform_ec-hyc_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Iberia_a320-200_planform_ec-hyc_arp.jpg/220px-Iberia_a320-200_planform_ec-hyc_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Iberia_a320-200_planform_ec-hyc_arp.jpg/330px-Iberia_a320-200_planform_ec-hyc_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Iberia_a320-200_planform_ec-hyc_arp.jpg/440px-Iberia_a320-200_planform_ec-hyc_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1814" data-file-height="1540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iberia_a320-200_planform_ec-hyc_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Planform view with flaps still partly extended, showing the 10.3 <a href="/wiki/Wing_aspect_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing aspect ratio">wing aspect ratio</a> and 25\xc2\xb0 <a href="/wiki/Wing_sweep" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing sweep">wing sweep</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Airbus A320 family are low-wing <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilever</a> <a href="/wiki/Monoplane" title="Monoplane">monoplanes</a> with a conventional <a href="/wiki/Empennage" title="Empennage">empennage</a> with a single <a href="/wiki/Vertical_stabilizer" title="Vertical stabilizer">vertical stabilizer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Wing_sweep" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing sweep">wing sweep</a> is 25 degrees. Compared to other airliners of the same class, the A320 features a wider single-aisle cabin of 3.95 metres (156&#160;in) outside diameter,<sup id="cite_ref-A320_specifications_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A320_specifications-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> compared to 3.8&#160;m (148&#160;in) of the Boeing 737 or <a href="/wiki/Boeing_757" title="Boeing 757">757</a>, and larger overhead bins. Its cargo hold can accommodate <a href="/wiki/Unit_Load_Device" class="mw-redirect" title="Unit Load Device">Unit Load Devices</a> containers.\n</p><p>The A320 <a href="/wiki/Airframe" title="Airframe">airframe</a> includes composite materials and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_alloy" title="Aluminium alloy">aluminium alloys</a> to save weight and reduce the total number of parts to decrease the maintenance costs.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Empennage" title="Empennage">tail assembly</a> is made almost entirely of such composites by CASA, who also builds the elevators, main landing gear doors, and rear fuselage parts.<sup id="cite_ref-NorrisWagner1999_10-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorrisWagner1999-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_deck">Flight deck</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Flight deck">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Turkish_Airlines_Airbus_A321_cockpit_closeup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Turkish_Airlines_Airbus_A321_cockpit_closeup.jpg/220px-Turkish_Airlines_Airbus_A321_cockpit_closeup.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Turkish_Airlines_Airbus_A321_cockpit_closeup.jpg/330px-Turkish_Airlines_Airbus_A321_cockpit_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Turkish_Airlines_Airbus_A321_cockpit_closeup.jpg/440px-Turkish_Airlines_Airbus_A321_cockpit_closeup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="780" data-file-height="520" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Turkish_Airlines_Airbus_A321_cockpit_closeup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The A320 <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a> has <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> controls</div></div></div>\n<p>It includes a full glass cockpit rather than the hybrid versions found in previous airliners. The A320\'s flight deck is equipped with <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Flight_Instrument_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic Flight Instrument System">Electronic Flight Instrument System</a> (EFIS) with side-stick controllers. The A320 features an <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Centralised_Aircraft_Monitor" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic Centralised Aircraft Monitor">Electronic Centralised Aircraft Monitor</a> (ECAM) which gives the flight crew information about all the systems of the aircraft. The only analogue instruments were the <a href="/wiki/Radio_direction_finder#Radio-magnetic_indicator_(RMI)" title="Radio direction finder">radio-magnetic indicator</a> and brake pressure indicator.\n</p><p>Since 2003, the A320 features <a href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_display" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquid crystal display">liquid crystal display</a> (LCD) units in its flight deck instead of the original <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathode ray tube">cathode ray tube</a> (CRT) displays. These include the main displays and the backup <a href="/wiki/Artificial_horizon" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial horizon">artificial horizon</a>, which was previously an analog display.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Airbus offers an avionics upgrade for older A320 aircraft, the In-Service Enhancement Package, to keep them updated.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Digital <a href="/wiki/Head-up_display" title="Head-up display">head-up displays</a> are available.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The A320 retained the <i>dark cockpit</i> (where an indicator is off when its system is running; useful for drawing attention to dysfunctions when an indicator is lit) from the A310, the first widebody designed to be operated without a flight engineer and influenced by <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Ziegler" title="Bernard Ziegler">Bernard Ziegler</a>, first Airbus CEO <a href="/wiki/Henri_Ziegler" title="Henri Ziegler">Henri Ziegler</a>\'s son.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fly-by-wire">Fly-by-wire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Fly-by-wire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The A320 is the world\'s first airliner with digital <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> (FBW) <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_flight_control_system" title="Aircraft flight control system">flight control system</a>: input commands through the <a href="/wiki/Side-stick" title="Side-stick">side-stick</a> are interpreted by flight control computers and transmitted to <a href="/wiki/Flight_control_surface" class="mw-redirect" title="Flight control surface">flight control surfaces</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Flight_envelope_protection" title="Flight envelope protection">flight envelope protection</a>; in the 1980s the computer-controlled dynamic system of the <a href="/wiki/Dassault_Mirage_2000" title="Dassault Mirage 2000">Dassault Mirage 2000</a> fighter cross-fertilised the Airbus team which tested FBW on an <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A300" title="Airbus A300">A300</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FG170220_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG170220-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> At its introduction, fly-by-wire and flight envelope protection was a new experience for many pilots.\n</p><p>All following Airbuses have similar human/machine interface and systems control philosophy to facilitate cross-type qualification with minimal training. For <a href="/wiki/Roger_B%C3%A9teille" title="Roger B\xc3\xa9teille">Roger B\xc3\xa9teille</a>, then Airbus president, introducing fly-by-wire with flight envelope protection was one of the most difficult decisions he had ever made, explaining: "Either we were going to be first with new technologies or we could not expect to be in the market."<sup id="cite_ref-FG170220_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG170220-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early A320s used the <a href="/wiki/Intel_80186" title="Intel 80186">Intel 80186</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motorola_68010" title="Motorola 68010">Motorola 68010</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 1988, the flight management computer contained six <a href="/wiki/Intel_80286" title="Intel 80286">Intel 80286</a> CPUs, running in three logical pairs, with 2.5 megabytes of memory.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engines">Engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A320_family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The suppliers providing <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">turbofan</a> engines for the A320 series are CFM International with the CFM56, <a href="/wiki/International_Aero_Engines" title="International Aero Engines">International Aero Engines</a> offering its V2500, and <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney">Pratt &amp; Whitney</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_PW6000" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney PW6000">PW6000</a> engines available only for the A318.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 242px"><div style="width: 242px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 240px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:AIR_BERLIN_(2560829519).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/AIR_BERLIN_%282560829519%29.jpg/360px-AIR_BERLIN_%282560829519%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="160" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/AIR_BERLIN_%282560829519%29.jpg/540px-AIR_BERLIN_%282560829519%29.jpg 1.5x, /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/CFM56" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM56">CFM56</a>, with an unmixed exhaust, is available on all variants\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215.33333333333px"><div style="width: 215.33333333333px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: class="gallerytext">\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/IAE_V2500" title="IAE V2500">IAE V2500</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_mixer" title="Exhaust mixer">mixed exhaust</a>, equips the larger variants\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 242px"><div style="width: 242px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 240px;"><div style="margin:0px
